<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_(title)" title="King (title)">King (title)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rex_(title)" title="Rex (title)">Rex (title)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Realm" title="Realm">Realm</a></div>
<p>The word "monarch" (Late Latin: <i lang="la">monarchia</i>) comes from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BC%CE%BF%CE%BD%CE%AC%CF%81%CF%87%CE%B7%CF%82" title="wikt:μονάρχης">μονάρχης</a></span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">monárkhēs</i></span>), derived from <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BC%CF%8C%CE%BD%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:μόνος">μόνος</a></span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">mónos</i></span>, "one, single") and <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%84%CF%81%CF%87%CF%89" title="wikt:ἄρχω">ἄρχω</a></span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">árkhō</i></span>, "to rule"): compare <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%84%CF%81%CF%87%CF%89%CE%BD" title="wikt:ἄρχων">ἄρχων</a></span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Archon" title="Archon">árkhōn</a></i></span>, "ruler, chief"). It referred to a single at least nominally absolute ruler. In current usage the word <i>monarchy</i> usually refers to a traditional system of hereditary rule, as elective monarchies are quite rare.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sumeriankinglist.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="589" data-file-width="320" decoding="async" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Sumeriankinglist.jpg/170px-Sumeriankinglist.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Sumeriankinglist.jpg/255px-Sumeriankinglist.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/Sumeriankinglist.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sumeriankinglist.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Weld-Blundell_Prism" title="Weld-Blundell Prism">Weld-Blundell Prism</a>, inscribed with the <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_King_List" title="Sumerian King List">Sumerian King List</a></div></div></div>
<p>The similar form of societal hierarchy known as <a href="/wiki/Chiefdom" title="Chiefdom">chiefdom</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribal_kingship" title="Tribal kingship">tribal kingship</a> is prehistoric. Chiefdoms provided the concept of state formation, which started with civilizations such as <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilization" title="Indus Valley civilization">Indus Valley civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kottak1991_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kottak1991-1">[1]</a></sup> In some parts of the world, chiefdoms became monarchies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoahenAjayi1986_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-BoahenAjayi1986-2">[2]</a></sup> Some of the oldest recorded and evidenced monarchies were <a href="/wiki/Narmer" title="Narmer">Narmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaoh</a> of Ancient Egypt c. 3100 BCE, and <a href="/wiki/Enmebaragesi" title="Enmebaragesi">Enmebaragesi</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> King of <a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">Kish</a> c. 2600 BCE.
</p><p>From earliest records, monarchs could be directly hereditary, while others were elected from among eligible members. With the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Egyptian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Indian" title="Ancient Indian">Indian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lugal" title="Lugal">Mesopotamian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudanic_languages" title="Sudanic languages">Sudanic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> reconstructed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_religion" title="Proto-Indo-European religion">Proto-Indo-European religion</a>, and others, the monarch held <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacral_kingship" title="Sacral kingship">sacral functions</a> directly connected to <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifice</a> and was sometimes identified with having <a href="/wiki/Imperial_cult" title="Imperial cult">divine ancestry</a>, possibly establishing a notion of the <a href="/wiki/Divine_right_of_kings" title="Divine right of kings">divine right of kings</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> identified monarchy as one of three "benign" basic forms of government (monarchy, <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Democracy#Origins" title="Democracy">democracy</a>), opposed to the three "malignant" basic forms of government (<a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyranny</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ochlocracy" title="Ochlocracy">ochlocracy</a>). The monarch in classical antiquity is often identified as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_(title)" title="King (title)">king</a>" or "ruler" (translating <i><a href="/wiki/Archon" title="Archon">archon</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Basileus" title="Basileus">basileus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rex_(title)" title="Rex (title)">rex</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrannos</a></i>, etc.) or as "<a href="/wiki/Queen_regnant" title="Queen regnant">queen</a>" (<i><a href="/wiki/Basilinna" title="Basilinna">basilinna</a></i>). Polybius originally understood monarchy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[note 1]</a></sup> as a component of republics, but since antiquity monarchy has contrasted with forms of republic, where executive power is wielded by free citizens and their assemblies. The 4th-century BCE Hindu text <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthasastra" title="Arthasastra">Arthasastra</a></i> laid out the ethics of monarchism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> In antiquity, some monarchies were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abolished_monarchy" title="Abolished monarchy">abolished</a> in favour of such assemblies in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a> (<a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>, 509 BCE), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Athens" title="Ancient Athens">Athens</a> (<a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">Athenian democracy</a>, 500 BCE).
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_map_1648.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1367" data-file-width="1894" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Europe_map_1648.PNG/240px-Europe_map_1648.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Europe_map_1648.PNG/360px-Europe_map_1648.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Europe_map_1648.PNG/480px-Europe_map_1648.PNG 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_map_1648.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of monarchies and republics in 1648</div></div></div>
<p>By the 17th century, monarchy was challenged by evolving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliamentarism" title="Parliamentarism">parliamentarism</a> e.g. through regional assemblies (such as the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Commonwealth" title="Icelandic Commonwealth">Icelandic Commonwealth</a>, the Swiss <i><a href="/wiki/Landsgemeinde" title="Landsgemeinde">Landsgemeinde</a></i> and later <i><a href="/wiki/Tagsatzung" title="Tagsatzung">Tagsatzung</a></i>, and the High Medieval <a href="/wiki/Medieval_commune" title="Medieval commune">communal movement</a> linked to the rise of medieval <a href="/wiki/Town_privileges" title="Town privileges">town privileges</a>) and by modern anti-monarchism e.g. of the temporary overthrow of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_monarchy" title="English monarchy">English monarchy</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a> in 1649, the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> of 1776 and the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> of 1789. One of many opponents of that trend was <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Dawbarn" title="Elizabeth Dawbarn">Elizabeth Dawbarn</a>, whose anonymous <i>Dialogue between Clara Neville and Louisa Mills, on Loyalty</i> (1794) features "silly Louisa, who admires liberty, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tom_Paine" title="Tom Paine">Tom Paine</a> and the US, [who is] lectured by Clara on God's approval of monarchy" and on the influence women can exert on men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since then advocacy of the abolition of a monarchy or respectively of <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republics</a> has been called <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a>, while the advocacy of monarchies is called <a href="/wiki/Monarchism" title="Monarchism">monarchism</a>. As such republics have become the opposing and alternative form of government to monarchy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M-W_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-M-W-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> despite some having seen <a href="/wiki/President_for_life#Similarity_to_a_monarch" title="President for life">infringements</a> through lifelong or even hereditary heads of state, such as in <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hermit_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hermit-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the rise of republicanism a diverse division between republicanism developed in the 19th-century politics (such as anti-monarchist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radicalism_(historical)" title="Radicalism (historical)">radicalism</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservativism" title="Conservativism">conservative</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> <a href="/wiki/Monarchism" title="Monarchism">monarchism</a>. In the following 20th century many countries <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abolished_monarchy" title="Abolished monarchy">abolished monarchy</a> and became republics, especially in the wake of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.
</p><p>Today forty-three sovereign nations in the world have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_current_sovereign_monarchs" title="List of current sovereign monarchs">monarch</a>, including fifteen <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">Commonwealth realms</a> that have <a href="/wiki/Charles_III" title="Charles III">Charles III</a> as the head of state. Most modern monarchs are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarch" title="Constitutional monarch">constitutional monarchs</a>, who retain a unique legal and ceremonial role but exercise limited or no political power under a constitution. Many are so-called <a href="/wiki/Crowned_republic" title="Crowned republic">crowned republics</a>, surviving particularly in small states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some nations, however, such as <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, the hereditary monarch has more political influence than any other single source of authority in the state, even if it is by a constitutional mandate.
</p><p>According to a 2020 study, monarchy arose as a system of governance because of an efficiency in governing large populations and expansive territories during periods when coordinating such populations was difficult. The authors argue that monarchy declined as an efficient regime type with innovations in communications and transportation technology, as the efficiency of monarchy relative to other regime types declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics_and_role">Characteristics and role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Characteristics and role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allan_Ramsay_-_King_George_III_in_coronation_robes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3801" data-file-width="2433" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Allan_Ramsay_-_King_George_III_in_coronation_robes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Allan_Ramsay_-_King_George_III_in_coronation_robes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Allan_Ramsay_-_King_George_III_in_coronation_robes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Allan_Ramsay_-_King_George_III_in_coronation_robes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Allan_Ramsay_-_King_George_III_in_coronation_robes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Allan_Ramsay_-_King_George_III_in_coronation_robes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allan_Ramsay_-_King_George_III_in_coronation_robes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III of the United Kingdom</a>, Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Allan_Ramsay_(artist)" title="Allan Ramsay (artist)">Allan Ramsay</a>, 1762.</div></div></div>
<p>Monarchies are associated with <a href="/wiki/Hereditary_monarchy" title="Hereditary monarchy">hereditary reign</a>, in which monarchs reign for life<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[note 2]</a></sup> and the responsibilities and power of the position pass to their child or another member of their family when they die. Most monarchs, both historically and in the modern-day, have been born and brought up within a <a href="/wiki/Royal_family" title="Royal family">royal family</a>, the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_household" title="Royal household">royal household</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noble_court" title="Noble court">court</a>. Growing up in a royal family (called a <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">dynasty</a> when it continues for several <a href="/wiki/Generation" title="Generation">generations</a>), <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">future monarchs</a> are often trained for their expected future responsibilities as monarch.
</p><p>Different systems of hereditary <a href="/wiki/Order_of_succession" title="Order of succession">succession</a> have been used, such as <a href="/wiki/Proximity_of_blood" title="Proximity of blood">proximity of blood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agnatic_seniority" title="Agnatic seniority">agnatic seniority</a> (<a href="/wiki/Salic_law" title="Salic law">Salic law</a>). While most monarchs in history have been male, many female monarchs also have reigned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> The term "<a href="/wiki/Queen_regnant" title="Queen regnant">queen regnant</a>" refers to a ruling monarch, while "<a href="/wiki/Queen_consort" title="Queen consort">queen consort</a>" refers to the wife of a reigning king. Rule may be hereditary in practice without being considered a monarchy: there have been some family dictatorships<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[note 3]</a></sup> (and also <a href="/wiki/Political_family" title="Political family">political families</a>) in many <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[note 4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The principal advantage of hereditary monarchy is the immediate continuity of leadership (as evidenced in the classic phrase "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_King_is_dead._Long_live_the_King!" title="The King is dead. Long live the King!">The King is dead. Long live the King!</a>").
</p><p>Some monarchies are not hereditary. In an <a href="/wiki/Elective_monarchy" title="Elective monarchy">elective monarchy</a>, monarchs are <a href="/wiki/Election" title="Election">elected</a> or appointed by some body (an <a href="/wiki/Electoral_college" title="Electoral college">electoral college</a>) for life or a defined period. Four elective monarchies exist today: <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> are 20th-century creations, while one (the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">papacy</a>) is ancient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Self-proclaimed_monarchy" title="Self-proclaimed monarchy">self-proclaimed monarchy</a> is established when a person claims the monarchy without any historical ties to a previous dynasty. There are examples of republican leaders who have proclaimed themselves monarchs: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon I of France</a> declared himself <a href="/wiki/List_of_French_monarchs" title="List of French monarchs">Emperor of the French</a> and ruled the <a href="/wiki/First_French_Empire" title="First French Empire">First French Empire</a> after having held the title of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Consul" title="First Consul">First Consul</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_French_Republic" title="First French Republic">French Republic</a> for five years from his seizing power in the coup of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/18_Brumaire" title="18 Brumaire">18 Brumaire</a>. President <a href="/wiki/Jean-B%C3%A9del_Bokassa" title="Jean-Bédel Bokassa">Jean-Bédel Bokassa</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a> declared himself Emperor of the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Empire" title="Central African Empire">Central African Empire</a> in 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shikai" title="Yuan Shikai">Yuan Shikai</a>, the first formal President of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republic of China</a>, crowned himself Emperor of the short-lived "<a href="/wiki/Empire_of_China_(1915%E2%80%931916)" title="Empire of China (1915–1916)">Empire of China</a>" a few years after the Republic of China was founded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Powers_of_the_monarch">Powers of the monarch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Powers of the monarch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9,_2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1386" data-file-width="1360" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg/200px-Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg/300px-Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg/400px-Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9,_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King <a href="/wiki/Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Salman of Saudi Arabia">Salman of Saudi Arabia</a> is an absolute monarch.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>In an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a>, the monarch rules as an <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocrat</a>, with absolute power over the state and government—for example, the right to <a href="/wiki/Rule_by_decree" title="Rule by decree">rule by decree</a>, promulgate <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">laws</a>, and impose <a href="/wiki/Punishment" title="Punishment">punishments</a>.</li>
<li>In a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>, the monarch's power is subject to a <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a>. In most current constitutional monarchies, the monarch is mainly a ceremonial <a href="/wiki/Figurehead" title="Figurehead">figurehead</a> <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbol</a> of national unity and state continuity. Although nominally <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereign</a>, the electorate (through the <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislature</a>) exercises political sovereignty. Constitutional monarchs' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_power" title="Political power">political power</a> is limited. Typical monarchical powers include granting <a href="/wiki/Pardon" title="Pardon">pardons</a>, granting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honour_system" title="Honour system">honours</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reserve_power" title="Reserve power">reserve powers</a>, e.g. to dismiss the <a href="/wiki/Prime_minister" title="Prime minister">prime minister</a>, refuse to dissolve parliament, or <a href="/wiki/Veto" title="Veto">veto</a> legislation ("withhold <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Assent" title="Royal Assent">Royal Assent</a>"). They often also have privileges of inviolability and <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_immunity" title="Sovereign immunity">sovereign immunity</a>. A monarch's powers and influence will depend on tradition, precedent, popular opinion, and <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a>.
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-constitutional_monarchy" title="Semi-constitutional monarchy">Semi-constitutional monarchies</a> exhibit fewer parliamentary powers or simply monarchs with more authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[17]</a></sup> The term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliamentary_monarchy" title="Parliamentary monarchy">parliamentary monarchy</a>" may be used to differentiate from semi-constitutional monarchies.</li></ul></li>
<li>Monarchical reign has often been linked with <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">military authority</a>. In the late <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a> several times deposed <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperors</a> and installed new emperors. Similarly, in the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ghilman" title="Ghilman">Ghilmans</a> (slave soldiers) deposed Caliphs once they became prominent, allowing new ones to come to power. The Hellenistic kings of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a> were elected by the army, which was similar in composition to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecclesia_(ancient_Athens)" title="Ecclesia (ancient Athens)">ecclesia</a></i> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democracies" title="Democracies">democracies</a>, the council of all free citizens; military service was often linked with citizenship among the male members of the royal house. The military has dominated the monarch in modern <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Japan" title="Medieval Japan">medieval Japan</a> (where a hereditary military chief, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dgun" title="Shōgun">shōgun</a></i>, was the <i>de facto</i> ruler, although the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Japanese emperor</a> nominally reigned). In <a href="/wiki/Italian_fascism" title="Italian fascism">Fascist Italy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Savoy" title="House of Savoy">Savoy</a> monarchy under King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_Emmanuel_III" title="Victor Emmanuel III">Victor Emmanuel III</a> coexisted with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Fascist_Party" title="Italian Fascist Party">Fascist</a> single-party rule of <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>; <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Romania" title="Kingdom of Romania">Romania</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Guard" title="Iron Guard">Iron Guard</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Greece_(Gl%C3%BCcksburg)" title="Kingdom of Greece (Glücksburg)">Greece</a> during the first months of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_military_junta_of_1967%E2%80%931974" title="Greek military junta of 1967–1974">Colonels' regime</a> were similar. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spain_under_Franco" title="Spain under Franco">Spain under Francisco Franco</a> was officially a monarchy, although there was no monarch on the throne. Upon his death, Franco was succeeded as head of state by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Bourbon" title="House of Bourbon">Bourbon</a> heir, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juan_Carlos_I_of_Spain" title="Juan Carlos I of Spain">Juan Carlos I</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_transition_to_democracy" title="Spanish transition to democracy">Spain became a democracy</a> with the king as a figurehead constitutional monarch.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Person_of_monarch">Person of monarch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Person of monarch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ruling-monarchs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="520" decoding="async" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ruling-monarchs.jpg/220px-Ruling-monarchs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ruling-monarchs.jpg/330px-Ruling-monarchs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ruling-monarchs.jpg/440px-Ruling-monarchs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ruling-monarchs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Postcard of ruling monarchs, taken in 1909  between February (accession of <a href="/wiki/Manuel_II_of_Portugal" title="Manuel II of Portugal">King Manuel II of Portugal</a>) and November (death of <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">Guangxu Emperor</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Most monarchies only have a single person acting as monarch at any given time, although two monarchs have ruled simultaneously in some countries, a situation known as <a href="/wiki/Diarchy" title="Diarchy">diarchy</a>. Historically this was the case in the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_city-state" title="Greek city-state">Greek city-state</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>. There are examples of joint sovereignty of spouses, parent and child or other relatives (such as <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William III</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_II" title="Mary II">Mary II</a> in the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">tsars</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivan_V_of_Russia" title="Ivan V of Russia">Ivan V of Russia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joanna_of_Castile" title="Joanna of Castile">Joanna of Castile</a>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Andorra" title="Andorra">Andorra</a> currently is the world's only constitutional diarchy, a co-principality. Located in the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a> between <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, it has two co-princes: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Urgell" title="Bishop of Urgell">bishop of Urgell</a> in Spain (a <a href="/wiki/Prince-bishop" title="Prince-bishop">prince-bishop</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/President_of_France" title="President of France">president of France</a> (derived <i>ex officio</i> from the French kings, who themselves inherited the title from the counts of Foix). It is the only case in which an independent country's (co-)monarch is <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratically</a> elected by the citizens of another country.
</p><p>In a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a>, separate independent states share the same person as monarch, but each realm retains separate laws and government. The fifteen separate <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">Commonwealth realms</a> are sometimes described as being in a personal union with King Charles III as monarch; however, they can also be described as being in a shared monarchy.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> may rule when the monarch is a <a href="/wiki/Minor_(law)" title="Minor (law)">minor</a>, absent, or debilitated.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Pretender" title="Pretender">pretender</a> is a claimant to an abolished throne or a throne already occupied by somebody else.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Abdication" title="Abdication">Abdication</a> is the act of formally giving up one's monarchical power and status.
</p><p>Monarchs may mark the ceremonial beginning of their reigns with a <a href="/wiki/Coronation" title="Coronation">coronation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Enthronement" title="Enthronement">enthronement</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_monarch">Role of monarch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Role of monarch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Monarchy, especially absolute monarchy, is sometimes linked to <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious</a> aspects; many monarchs once claimed the right to rule by the will of a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_Right_of_Kings" title="Divine Right of Kings">Divine Right of Kings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">Mandate of Heaven</a>), or a special connection to a deity (<a href="/wiki/Sacred_king" title="Sacred king">sacred king</a>), or even purported to be divine kings, or <a href="/wiki/Incarnation" title="Incarnation">incarnations</a> of deities themselves (<a href="/wiki/Imperial_cult" title="Imperial cult">imperial cult</a>). Many European monarchs have been styled <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fidei_defensor" title="Fidei defensor">Fidei defensor</a></i> (Defender of the Faith); some hold official positions relating to the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a> or established church.
</p><p>In the Western political tradition, a morally based, balanced monarchy was stressed as the ideal form of government, and little attention was paid to modern-day ideals of egalitarian democracy: e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Thomas_Aquinas" title="Saint Thomas Aquinas">Saint Thomas Aquinas</a> unapologetically declared: "Tyranny is wont to occur not less but more frequently on the basis of polyarchy [rule by many, i.e. oligarchy or democracy] than on the basis of monarchy." (<i>On Kingship</i>). However, Thomas Aquinas also stated that the ideal monarchical system would also have at lower levels of government both an aristocracy and elements of democracy in order to create a balance of power. The monarch would also be subject to both natural and divine law, and to the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Church</a> in matters of religion.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante Alighieri</a>'s <i>De Monarchia</i>, a spiritualised, imperial Catholic monarchy is strongly promoted according to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghibelline" title="Ghibelline">Ghibelline</a> world-view in which the "royal religion of <a href="/wiki/Melchizedek" title="Melchizedek">Melchizedek</a>" is emphasised against the priestly claims of the rival papal ideology.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/King_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="King of Saudi Arabia">king</a> is a head of state who is both the absolute monarch of the country and the <a href="/wiki/Custodian_of_the_Two_Holy_Mosques" title="Custodian of the Two Holy Mosques">Custodian of the Two Holy Mosques</a> of Islam (خادم الحرمين الشريفين).
</p><p>The roles of monarchs can overlap with other monarchies through <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dynastic_union" title="Dynastic union">dynastic union</a>, with maybe becoming institutional <a href="/wiki/Real_union" title="Real union">real union</a> and possibly a larger <a href="/wiki/Federal_monarchy" title="Federal monarchy">federal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Composite_monarchy" title="Composite monarchy">composite</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unitary_monarchy" title="Unitary monarchy">unitary</a> monarchy, <a href="/wiki/Realm" title="Realm">realm</a> and state.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles_of_monarchs">Titles of monarchs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Titles of monarchs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Composite_monarchy" title="Composite monarchy">Composite monarchy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T%C3%A9wodros_II_-_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="377" decoding="async" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/T%C3%A9wodros_II_-_2.jpg/220px-T%C3%A9wodros_II_-_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/T%C3%A9wodros_II_-_2.jpg/330px-T%C3%A9wodros_II_-_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f4/T%C3%A9wodros_II_-_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T%C3%A9wodros_II_-_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tewodros_II" title="Tewodros II">Tewodros II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Ethiopia" title="Emperor of Ethiopia">Emperor of Ethiopia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Monarchs can have various <a href="/wiki/Title" title="Title">titles</a>. Common European titles of monarchs (in that hierarchical order of nobility) are <a href="/wiki/Emperor" title="Emperor">emperor</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress" title="Empress">empress</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>: <a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">imperator</a> or imperatrix), <a href="/wiki/King" title="King">king</a> or <a href="/wiki/Queen_regnant" title="Queen regnant">queen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_duke" title="Grand duke">grand duke</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_duchess" title="Grand duchess">grand duchess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince" title="Prince">prince</a> or <a href="/wiki/Princess" title="Princess">princess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke" title="Duke">duke</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duchess" title="Duchess">duchess</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[18]</a></sup> Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_modern" title="Early modern">early modern</a> European titles (especially in German states) included <a href="/wiki/Prince-elector" title="Prince-elector">elector</a> (German: <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Kurfürst</i></span>, Prince-Elector, literally "electing prince"), <a href="/wiki/Margrave" title="Margrave">margrave</a> (German: <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Markgraf</i></span>, equivalent to the French title <i>marquis</i>, literally "count of the borderland"), and <a href="/wiki/Burgrave" title="Burgrave">burgrave</a> (German: <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Burggraf</i></span>, literally "count of the castle"). Lesser titles include <a href="/wiki/Count" title="Count">count</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Princely_count" title="Princely count">princely count</a>. Slavic titles include <a href="/wiki/Knyaz" title="Knyaz">knyaz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">tsar</a> (ц︢рь) or <a href="/wiki/Tsarina" title="Tsarina">tsaritsa</a> (царица), a word derived from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman imperial</a> title <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar" title="Caesar">Caesar</a></i>.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>, titles of monarchs include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a> (successor to the Islamic prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> and a leader of the entire Muslim community), <a href="/wiki/Padishah" title="Padishah">padishah</a> (emperor), <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">sultan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sultana_(title)" title="Sultana (title)">sultana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">shâhanshâh</a> (emperor), <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">shah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malik" title="Malik">malik</a> (king) or <a href="/wiki/Malik" title="Malik">malikah</a> (queen), <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">emir</a> (commander, prince) or <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">emira</a> (princess), <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">sheikh</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">sheikha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">imam</a> (used in <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>). East Asian titles of monarchs include <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China"><i>huángdì</i></a> (emperor) or <i>nǚhuáng</i> (empress regnant), <a href="/wiki/Son_of_Heaven" title="Son of Heaven"><i>tiānzǐ</i></a> (son of heaven), <i><a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">tennō</a></i> (emperor) or <i><a href="/wiki/Empress_of_Japan" title="Empress of Japan">josei tennō</a></i> (empress regnant), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Korea" title="King of Korea">wang</a></i> (king) or <i>yeowang</i> (queen regnant), <i><a href="/wiki/Korean_Empire" title="Korean Empire">hwangje</a></i> (emperor) or <i><a href="/wiki/Korean_Empire" title="Korean Empire">yeoje</a></i> (empress regnant). South Asian and South East Asian titles included <i><a href="/wiki/Maharaja" title="Maharaja">mahārāja</a></i> (high king) or <i>maharani</i> (high queen), <i><a href="/wiki/Raja" title="Raja">raja</a></i> (king) and <i><a href="/wiki/Rana_(title)" title="Rana (title)">rana</a></i> (king) or <i><a href="/wiki/Rani" title="Rani">rani</a></i> (queen) and <i>ratu</i> (South East Asian queen). Historically, <a href="/wiki/Mongolic_languages" title="Mongolic languages">Mongolic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a> monarchs have used the title <i><a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">khan</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Khagan" title="Khagan">khagan</a></i> (emperor) or <i><a href="/wiki/Khatun" title="Khatun">khatun</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Khanum" title="Khanum">khanum</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptian</a> monarchs have used the title <i><a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a></i> for men and women. In <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopian Empire</a>, monarchs used title <i><a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Ethiopia" title="Emperor of Ethiopia">nəgusä nägäst</a></i> (king of kings) or <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Ethiopia" title="Emperor of Ethiopia">nəgəstä nägäst</a> (queen of kings).
</p><p>Many monarchs are addressed with particular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Style_(manner_of_address)" title="Style (manner of address)">styles</a> or manners of address, like "<a href="/wiki/Majesty" title="Majesty">Majesty</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Royal_Highness" title="Royal Highness">Royal Highness</a>", "<a href="/wiki/By_the_Grace_of_God" title="By the Grace of God">By the Grace of God</a>", <i><a href="/wiki/Amir_al-Mu%27minin" title="Amir al-Mu'minin">Amīr al-Mu'minīn</a></i> ("Leader of the Faithful"), <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_sultans_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="List of sultans of the Ottoman Empire">Hünkar-i Khanedan-i Âl-i Osman</a></i>, "Sovereign of the Sublime House of Osman"), <i><a href="/wiki/Malay_styles_and_titles" title="Malay styles and titles">Duli Yang Maha Mulia Seri Paduka Baginda</a></i> ("<a href="/wiki/Majesty" title="Majesty">Majesty</a>"), <i>Jeonha</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Majesty" title="Majesty">Majesty</a>"), <i>Tennō Heika</i> (literally "His Majesty the heavenly sovereign"), <i>Bìxià</i> ("Bottom of the Steps").
</p><p>Sometimes titles are used to express claims to territories that are not held in fact (for example, <a href="/wiki/English_claims_to_the_French_throne" title="English claims to the French throne">English claims to the French throne</a>), or titles not recognised (<a href="/wiki/Antipope" title="Antipope">antipopes</a>). Also, after a monarchy is deposed, often former monarchs and their descendants are given alternative titles (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Portugal" title="King of Portugal">King of Portugal</a> was given the hereditary title <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Braganza" title="Duke of Braganza">Duke of Braganza</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-sovereign_monarchies">Non-sovereign monarchies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Non-sovereign monarchies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-sovereign_monarchy" title="Non-sovereign monarchy">Non-sovereign monarchy</a></div>
<p>A non-sovereign monarchy is one where the monarch is subject to a temporal authority higher than their own. Some are dependent on other powers (see <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puppet_state" title="Puppet state">puppet state</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a>). In the British colonial era, <a href="/wiki/Indirect_rule" title="Indirect rule">indirect rule</a> under a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramount_power" title="Paramount power">paramount power</a> existed, such as the <a href="/wiki/Princely_state" title="Princely state">princely states</a> under the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, the ancient kingdoms and <a href="/wiki/Chiefdom" title="Chiefdom">chiefdoms</a> that were met by the colonialists when they first arrived on the continent are now constitutionally protected as regional or sectional entities.
</p><p>Furthermore, in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, though the hundreds of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nigerian_traditional_states" title="List of Nigerian traditional states">sub-regional polities</a> that exist there are not provided for in the current constitution, they are nevertheless legally recognised aspects of the structure of governance that operates in the nation. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a> city-state of <a href="/wiki/Akure_Kingdom" title="Akure Kingdom">Akure</a> in south-western Nigeria is something of an elective monarchy: its reigning <i><a href="/wiki/Oba_(ruler)" title="Oba (ruler)">Oba</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deji_of_Akure" title="Deji of Akure">Deji</a></i> has to be chosen by an electoral college of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nigerian_chieftaincy" title="Nigerian chieftaincy">nobles</a> from amongst a finite collection of royal princes of the realm upon the death or removal of an incumbent.
</p><p>In addition to these five countries, non-sovereign monarchies of varied sizes and complexities exist all over the rest of the continent of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statehood">Statehood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Statehood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Monarchies pre-date <a href="/wiki/Polity" title="Polity">polities</a> like <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation states</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup> and even <a href="/wiki/Territorial_state" title="Territorial state">territorial states</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nation</a> or constitution is not necessary in a monarchy since a person, the monarch, binds the separate territories and <a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(political)" title="Legitimacy (political)">political legitimacy</a> (e.g. in personal union) together.
</p><p>Monarchies, though, have applied <a href="/wiki/National_symbol" title="National symbol">state symbols</a> like <a href="/wiki/Insignia" title="Insignia">insignia</a> or abstracts like the concept of <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the Crown</a> to create a state identity, which is to be carried and occupied by the monarch, but represents the monarchy even in absence and <a href="#Succession">succession</a> of the monarch.
</p><p>Nevertheless, monarchies can also be bound to <a href="/wiki/Territory" title="Territory">territories</a> (e.g., the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Norway" title="King of Norway">King of Norway</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Popular_monarchy" title="Popular monarchy">peoples</a> (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Belgium" title="Monarchy of Belgium">King of the Belgians</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession">Succession</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Succession">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hereditary_monarchies">Hereditary monarchies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Hereditary monarchies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:European_monarchies_by_succession.svg"><img alt="Political Map of Europe with Monarchical states colour-coded" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="295" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/European_monarchies_by_succession.svg/210px-European_monarchies_by_succession.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/European_monarchies_by_succession.svg/315px-European_monarchies_by_succession.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/European_monarchies_by_succession.svg/420px-European_monarchies_by_succession.svg.png 2x" width="210"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:European_monarchies_by_succession.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Current European monarchies by succession method: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#018571; color:black;font-size:94%;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_primogeniture" title="Absolute primogeniture">Absolute primogeniture</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#dfc27d; color:black;font-size:94%;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Male-preference_cognatic_primogeniture" title="Male-preference cognatic primogeniture">Male-preference cognatic primogeniture</a>, to be changed to absolute primogeniture</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#a6611a; color:white;font-size:94%;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Male-preference_cognatic_primogeniture" title="Male-preference cognatic primogeniture">Male-preference cognatic primogeniture</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#000000; color:white;font-size:94%;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agnatic_primogeniture" title="Agnatic primogeniture">Agnatic primogeniture</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f5f5f5; color:black;font-size:94%;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Elective_monarchy" title="Elective monarchy">Elective</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Hereditary_monarchy" title="Hereditary monarchy">hereditary monarchy</a>, the position of monarch is inherited according to a statutory or customary <a href="/wiki/Order_of_succession" title="Order of succession">order of succession</a>, usually within one <a href="/wiki/Royal_family" title="Royal family">royal family</a> tracing its origin through a historical <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">dynasty</a> or bloodline. This usually means that the heir to the throne is known well in advance of becoming monarch to ensure a smooth succession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">Primogeniture</a>, in which the eldest child of the monarch is first in line to become monarch, is the most common system in hereditary monarchy. The order of succession is usually affected by rules on gender. Historically "agnatic primogeniture" or "patrilineal primogeniture" was favoured, that is inheritance according to seniority of birth among the sons of a monarch or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paterfamilias" title="Paterfamilias">head of family</a>, with sons and their male issue inheriting before brothers and their issue, and <a href="/wiki/Patrilineality" title="Patrilineality">male-line</a> males inheriting before females of the male line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy-26">[22]</a></sup> This is the same as semi-Salic primogeniture. Complete exclusion of females from <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">dynastic</a> succession is commonly referred to as application of the <a href="/wiki/Salic_law" title="Salic law">Salic law</a> (see <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terra_salica" title="Terra salica">Terra salica</a></i>).
</p><p>Before primogeniture was enshrined in European law and tradition, kings would often secure the succession by having their successor (usually their eldest son) crowned during their own lifetime, so for a time there would be two kings in <a href="/wiki/Coregency" title="Coregency">coregency</a>—a senior king and a junior king. Examples were <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Young_King" title="Henry the Young King">Henry the Young King</a> of England and the early <a href="/wiki/House_of_Capet" title="House of Capet">Direct Capetians</a> in France. Sometimes, however, primogeniture can operate through the female line.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saksen-Koburg_Leopold-2a.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1476" data-file-width="1305" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Saksen-Koburg_Leopold-2a.jpeg/190px-Saksen-Koburg_Leopold-2a.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Saksen-Koburg_Leopold-2a.jpeg/285px-Saksen-Koburg_Leopold-2a.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Saksen-Koburg_Leopold-2a.jpeg/380px-Saksen-Koburg_Leopold-2a.jpeg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saksen-Koburg_Leopold-2a.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_the_Belgians" title="King of the Belgians">King</a> <a href="/wiki/Leopold_I_of_Belgium" title="Leopold I of Belgium">Leopold I</a>, an elected founder of the hereditary <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Belgium" title="Monarchy of Belgium">monarchy of Belgium</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1980, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> became the first European monarchy to declare equal (full cognatic) primogeniture, meaning that the eldest child of the monarch, whether female or male, ascends to the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[23]</a></sup> Other kingdoms (such as the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> in 1983, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> in 1990, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> in 1991, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> in 2009, and <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup> in 2011) have since followed suit. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> adopted absolute (equal) primogeniture (subject to the claims of existing heirs) on April 25, 2013, following <a href="/wiki/Perth_Agreement" title="Perth Agreement">agreement by the prime ministers of the sixteen Commonwealth Realms</a> at the 22nd Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the absence of children, the next most senior member of the collateral line (for example, a younger sibling of the previous monarch) becomes monarch. In complex cases, this can mean that there are closer blood relatives to the deceased monarch than the next in line according to primogeniture. This has often led, especially in Europe in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, to conflict between the principle of primogeniture and the principle of <a href="/wiki/Proximity_of_blood" title="Proximity of blood">proximity of blood</a>.
</p><p>Other hereditary systems of succession included <a href="/wiki/Tanistry" title="Tanistry">tanistry</a>, which is semi-elective and gives weight to merit and <a href="/wiki/Agnatic_seniority" title="Agnatic seniority">Agnatic seniority</a>. In some monarchies, such as <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, succession to the throne first passes to the monarch's next eldest brother, and only after that to the monarch's children (agnatic seniority). However, on June 21, 2017, King Salman of Saudi Arabi revolted against this style of monarchy and elected his son to inherit the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elective_monarchies">Elective monarchies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Elective monarchies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pope_Francis_in_March_2013_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="354" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Pope_Francis_in_March_2013_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Pope_Francis_in_March_2013_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Pope_Francis_in_March_2013_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Pope_Francis_in_March_2013_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Pope_Francis_in_March_2013_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Pope_Francis_in_March_2013_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pope_Francis_in_March_2013_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a>, Sovereign of the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City State</a></div></div></div>
<p>In an <a href="/wiki/Elective_monarchy" title="Elective monarchy">elective monarchy</a>, monarchs are <a href="/wiki/Election" title="Election">elected</a> or appointed by somebody (an <a href="/wiki/Electoral_college" title="Electoral college">electoral college</a>) for life or a defined period, but then reign like any other monarch. There is no popular vote involved in elective monarchies, as the elective body usually consists of a small number of eligible people. Historical examples of elective monarchy are the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperors</a> (chosen by <a href="/wiki/Prince-elector" title="Prince-elector">prince-electors</a> but often coming from the same dynasty) and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_elections_in_Poland" title="Royal elections in Poland">free election</a> of kings of the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin the Short</a> (father of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>) was elected <a href="/wiki/List_of_Frankish_kings" title="List of Frankish kings">King of the Franks</a> by an assembly of Frankish leading men; nobleman <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_August_Poniatowski" title="Stanisław August Poniatowski">Stanisław August Poniatowski</a> of Poland was an elected king, as was <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I_of_Denmark" title="Frederick I of Denmark">Frederick I of Denmark</a>. <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a> also had elective monarchies.
</p><p>Six forms of elective monarchies exist today. The <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">pope</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> (who rules as <a href="/wiki/Sovereign" title="Sovereign">Sovereign</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City State</a>) is <a href="/wiki/Papal_conclave" title="Papal conclave">elected</a> for life by the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Cardinals" title="College of Cardinals">College of Cardinals</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_Military_Order_of_Malta" title="Sovereign Military Order of Malta">Sovereign Military Order of Malta</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Princes_and_Grand_Masters_of_the_Sovereign_Military_Order_of_Malta" title="List of Princes and Grand Masters of the Sovereign Military Order of Malta">Prince and Grand Master</a> is elected for life tenure by the Council Complete of State from within its members. In <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, the federal king, called the <a href="/wiki/Yang_di-Pertuan_Agong" title="Yang di-Pertuan Agong">Yang di-Pertuan Agong</a> or Paramount Ruler, is elected for a five-year term from among and by the hereditary rulers (mostly <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">sultans</a>) of nine of the federation's constitutive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/States_of_Malaysia" title="States of Malaysia">states</a>, all on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malay_peninsula" title="Malay peninsula">Malay peninsula</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> also chooses its federal leaders from among emirs of the federated states. Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Andorra" title="Andorra">Andorra</a> has a unique constitutional arrangement as one of its heads of state is the President of the <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French Republic</a> in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Co-Princes_of_Andorra" title="Co-Princes of Andorra">Co-Prince</a>. This is the only instance in the world where the monarch of a state is elected by the citizens of a different country. In New Zealand, the Maori King, head of the Kingitanga Movement, is elected by a council of Maori elders at the funeral of their predecessor, which is also where their coronation takes place. All of the Heads of the Maori King Movement have been descendants of the first Maori King, Potatau Te Wherowhero, who was elected and became King in June 1858. The current monarch is King <a href="/wiki/T%C5%ABheitia_Paki" title="Tūheitia Paki">Tuheitia Potatau Te Wherowhero VII</a>, who was elected and became King on 21 August 2006, the same day as the funeral of his mother, <a href="/wiki/Te_Atairangikaahu" title="Te Atairangikaahu">Te Arikinui Dame Te Atairangikaahu</a>, the first Maori Queen. As well as being King and head of the Kingitanga Movement, King Tuheitia is also <i>ex officio</i> the Paramount Chief of the Waikato-Tainui tribe.
</p><p>Appointment by the current monarch is another system, used in <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>. It also was used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Russia" title="Imperial Russia">Imperial Russia</a>; however, it was soon changed to semi-Salic because the instability of the appointment system resulted in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Russia_(1721%E2%80%9396)" title="History of Russia (1721–96)">an age of palace revolutions</a>. In this system, the monarch chooses the successor, who is always his relative.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Jure_uxoris" title="Jure uxoris">jure uxoris</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_ways_of_succession">Other ways of succession</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Other ways of succession">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Order_of_succession" title="Order of succession">Order of succession</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-proclaimed_monarchy" title="Self-proclaimed monarchy">Self-proclaimed monarchy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(political)" title="Legitimacy (political)">Legitimacy (political)</a></div>
<p>Other ways to success a monarchy can be through claiming alternative votes (e.g. as in the case of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Western Schism</a>), claims of a mandate to rule (e.g. a popular or <a href="/wiki/Divine_right_of_kings" title="Divine right of kings">divine mandate</a>), <a href="/wiki/Military_occupation" title="Military occupation">military occupation</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a>, a will of the previous monarch or treaties between factions inside and outside of a monarchy (e.g. as in the case of the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Spanish_Succession" title="War of the Spanish Succession">War of the Spanish Succession</a>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="By_accession">By accession</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: By accession">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Enthronement" title="Enthronement">Enthronement</a></div>
<p>The legitimacy and authorities of monarchs are often <a href="/wiki/Proclamation" title="Proclamation">proclaimed</a> and recognized through occupying and being <a href="/wiki/Investiture" title="Investiture">invested</a> with <a href="/wiki/Insignia" title="Insignia">insignia</a>, seats, <a href="/wiki/Deed" title="Deed">deeds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Title" title="Title">titles</a>, like in the course of <a href="/wiki/Coronation" title="Coronation">coronations</a>.
</p><p>This is especially employed to legitimize and settle disputed successions, changes in ways of succession, status of a monarch (e.g. as in the case of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Privilegium_maius" title="Privilegium maius">privilegium maius</a> deed) or new monarchies altogether (e.g. as in the case of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Napoleon_I" title="Coronation of Napoleon I">Coronation of Napoleon I</a>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dynasties">Dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Succession is often based on the expected continuation of a <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">dynastic period</a> or association in a <a href="/wiki/Dynastic_union" title="Dynastic union">dynastic union</a>, which is sometimes <a href="/wiki/War_of_succession" title="War of succession">challenged</a> by diverging <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(anthropology)" title="Lineage (anthropology)">lineage</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Legitimists_(disambiguation)" title="Legitimists (disambiguation)">legitimism</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession_crisis">Succession crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Succession crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Succession_crisis" title="Succession crisis">Succession crisis</a></div>
<p>In cases of succession challenges it can be instrumental for <a href="/wiki/Pretender" title="Pretender">pretenders</a> to secure or install <a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(political)" title="Legitimacy (political)">legitimacy</a> through the above, for example proof of accession like insignia, through treaties or a claim of a divine mandate to rule (e.g. by <a href="/wiki/Hong_Xiuquan" title="Hong Xiuquan">Hong Xiuquan</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Heavenly_Kingdom" title="Taiping Heavenly Kingdom">Taiping Heavenly Kingdom</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_monarchies">Current monarchies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Current monarchies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_current_monarchs_of_sovereign_states" title="List of current monarchs of sovereign states">List of current monarchs of sovereign states</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1086701767" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Multiple_issues plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-multiple_issues compact-ambox" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><div class="multiple-issues-text mw-collapsible"><b>This section has multiple issues.</b> Please help <b><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Monarchy&amp;action=edit">improve it</a></b> or discuss these issues on the <b><a href="/wiki/Talk:Monarchy" title="Talk:Monarchy">talk page</a></b>. <small><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove these template messages</a>)</i></small>

<table align="center">
<tbody><tr>
<td><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Monarchies.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="381" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/World_Monarchies.svg/750px-World_Monarchies.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/World_Monarchies.svg/1125px-World_Monarchies.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/World_Monarchies.svg/1500px-World_Monarchies.svg.png 2x" width="750"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Monarchies.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#AA0000; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">Absolute monarchies</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#D45500; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">Semi-constitutional monarchies</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#285F27; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">Parliamentary monarchies</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#008000; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/List_of_monarchies#Subnational_monarchies" title="List of monarchies">Subnational monarchies</a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>

<th>Systems of government
</th></tr>
